Chapter 1

CHAPTER 1

Havana’s harbor was a mouth full of rotten teeth. Sun-bleached Spanish galleons, their hulls splintered, listed against the dock like drunks, still waiting for cargo that would never come. A customs officer, sweating through his moth-eaten uniform, spent his mornings counting the same three crates of porcelain chamber pots, each painted with the face of a forgotten king.



Don Eusebio was in the middle of his midday ritual: a game of solitaire where the kings were all missing, replaced by water-stained cards depicting local saints of dubious provenance. He used the queen of spades as the General, a card so soft at the edges it felt like felt. He was contemplating the legality of moving a two of hearts when the postman shoved the crate across his desk, scattering the saints.

“For you. Smells like a carpenter’s remorse.”

Don Eusebio peered at the address. “It says ‘Municipal Palace.’”

“The Palace,” the postman said, wiping his mustache with the back of his wrist, “has been a parrot sanctuary since the earthquake of ’78. The mayor lives above the bakery. You are the closest thing we have to bureaucracy.”

This was irrefutable. Don Eusebio thanked him with a nod that cost him some dignity and waited for the man to leave. The crate sat there, humming a low, wooden frequency. He did not open it immediately. He first finished his game, arranging the saints in a pyramid that collapsed twice. He drank his té de tilo until it was cold. He watched a gecko track a moth across the water-stained ceiling. Only when the cathedral bell tolled three, a sound that always arrived late and breathless, did he fetch a crowbar from beneath a pile of census records from 1912.

The nails came out with a shriek that seemed to offend the very air. The sawdust was fine, blond, and it puffed into the room’s thick heat like golden smoke. He pushed it aside, his fingers encountering something cold, smooth, and unmistakably cranial. He lifted it out.

It was a handsome head, preserved with a skill that bordered on the insulting. The skin was a taught, waxy parchment, the color of old violin wood. The mustache—a magnificent, silver-bristled affair—was perfectly combed. The eyes were closed, the expression one of profound, administrative disappointment. It was, without question, General Íñigo de Alba y Merced, Liberator of the Province, Scourge of the Northern Frontier, and First Magistrate of the Republic, who had died one hundred and forty-seven years ago in a shipwreck off a coast that had since been swallowed by silt.

Don Eusebio placed the head on the desk, facing the window. He sat down. He and the General regarded the same view: the plaza, where the statue of the General—headless since the student protests of ’69—stood in a permanent state of botanical surprise, its shoulders sprouting bougainvillea.

“Well,” Don Eusebio said to the head. “You’ve taken the long way round.”

The head, predictably, did not answer. But in the silence, Don Eusebio felt the first tremor of the absurdity to come. A head in a box was a problem. A head with historical significance was a crisis. A head that belonged to a statue currently being used as a trellis was a cosmic joke.

He did what any sensible functionary in a town forgotten by time would do: he began to fill out forms. He selected the triplicate carbon-paper form B-7, “Unexpected Patrimonial Acquisition.” Under “Description of Item,” he wrote: Cranial remains, male, ca. 19th century, notable mustache. Under “Provenance,” he wrote: Postal service, unsolicited. Under “Recommended Action,” he paused. He wrote: Consultation with relevant authorities. Then he scratched it out. He wrote: Reunification with corresponding body. He scratched that out too. Finally, he wrote: Custodial holding pending meteorological clarification.

He was sanding the carbon copies with a blotter when the door opened again. It was Father Benítez, whose cassock was the grey of street dust and who carried with him the scent of slow-burning wax and existential doubt.

“Eusebio, the boys are saying you’ve received a parcel of contraband cheese. I’ve come to levy the spiritual tax.” His joke died in his throat as he saw the head. “Ah.”

“Íñigo de Alba,” Don Eusebio said, by way of explanation.

Father Benítez approached, circled the desk, and leaned in. He was close enough to kiss the General’s waxy forehead. “The preservation is heretical.”

“It’s professional.”

“Where is the rest of him?”

“The sea, presumably. Or a collector’s vault in Zurich. The crate is from a forwarding agency in Rotterdam. It passed through… let’s see.” Don Eusebio lifted the crate lid, squinted at the stamps. “Miami, Panama, a place called San Something that has a postmark so blurred it might as well be a Rorschach test, and finally our glorious capital, which took only six months to send it here.”

Father Benítez made the sign of the cross, not out of piety but punctuation. “This is a political act.”

“It’s a postal act.”

“Nothing arrives here by accident, Eusebio. Especially not a head. This is a message. A grenade rolled into our siesta.” The priest’s eyes gleamed with a familiar, weary paranoia. “Who sent it? The central government? The exhumation commission? The tourism ministry? Is this the preamble to a visit?”

Don Eusebio considered the head. The General’s lips were sewn shut with fine, almost invisible thread. “I think it’s the preamble to a paperwork nightmare. I now have to account for a national hero’s head. I need a storage solution. A box, a shelf… it can’t go next to the ceramic shards. It’s disrespectful.”

“Put it back in the crate.”

“And do what? Mark it ‘Return to Sender’? The return address is a bank in Liechtenstein.”

They were interrupted by a third presence. A shadow filled the doorway, then resolved itself into the substantial form of Remedios the Widow, who owned the cantina across the plaza and most of the viable opinions in town. She carried a clay pot of black beans that steamed with an authority the Municipal Palace lacked.

“I brought you lunch because you forget to eat and then you get melancholic and your paperwork suffers,” she announced, then saw the head. She did not startle. She set the pot down on a stack of land dispute files and leaned in, hands on her broad hips. “Huh. He looks better than the last time I saw him.”

“You’ve never seen him,” Father Benítez said.

“I saw the statue before the students decapitated it. Same expression. As if he’d just bitten into a lemon and discovered principle.” She poked the General’s temple with a work-rough finger. “Wax. Good wax. European. This is an expensive problem.”

“Why is it my problem?” Don Eusebio moaned, but it was a formality. He already knew. He was the notary. The keeper of records. The man who filed what happened. The head had happened, and now it was his.

Remedios took charge, as she did of all things that entered her gravitational field. “First, we don’t tell anyone. The governor will want it for a photo opportunity. The university will want to study it and lose an ear. The tourists, if we ever get any, will want to take selfies. Second, we find out why it’s here. A head doesn’t travel a century and a half for a holiday.”

“How do you propose we do that?” Father Benítez asked, already resigned to her plan.

“We ask,” she said, as if it were obvious.

She turned the head around to face them. The three of them stared at the serene, waxen face. The room seemed to hold its breath. Somewhere, a donkey brayed, a sound like a broken pump organ.

“Well?” Remedios said to the head. “Speak up. The postal fee has been paid.”

The General, of course, was silent. But in that silence, a conspiracy was born. Not a conspiracy of power, but of custodianship. The head was now theirs. A relic of a past so distant it had curdled into myth, dumped on their doorstep like an orphan. Don Eusebio felt the weight of it, the absurd responsibility. He was no longer just a notary with a tremor. He was the guardian of a national symbol that nobody had asked for.

“We’ll need a ledger,” he said, his voice assuming a formal tone he used for reading aloud the minutes of meetings that never happened. “A dedicated ledger. For observations. Condition reports. Any… communications.”

“Communications?” Father Benítez raised an eyebrow.

“If it starts to weep, or grow mold, or if the lips part. We must document it.”

Remedios nodded, practical. “I’ll bring a clean cloth from the cantina. We can’t have him gathering dust. It’s undignified.” She looked at the head, and her gaze softened, just for a moment. “Poor bastard. Lost his body, lost his statue, ends up here. We’re all just waiting for the right crate, aren’t we?”

Don Eusebio opened the new ledger. On the first line, in his meticulous script, he wrote:

Tuesday. 3:17 p.m. Subject received via postal service. Condition: stable. Mustache: impeccable. Sentiment in room: bewildered custodianship. Action taken: formation of unofficial committee. Note: The gecko on the ceiling appears interested.

He closed the book. The head sat on the desk, a paperweight for a nation that had blown away. Outside, the heat pressed down on the plaza, making the air above the headless statue shimmer. The siesta was over. The problem had arrived.

“We should name him,” Remedios said. “Can’t just call him ‘the head.’ It’s rude.”

“He has a name,” Father Benítez sighed. “General Íñigo de Alba y Merced.”

“Too long. Iñigo. We’ll call him Iñigo.” She patted the waxen cheek. “You’ll get used to it.”

Don Eusebio looked from the widow to the priest to the severed head of a liberator. He felt a laugh bubbling up, a dry, silent thing that got stuck in his chest. This was the inheritance. Not gold, not glory, not a legacy of bronze. A head in a box, a committee of three, and a ledger that would fill with the quiet, bizarre minutes of their guardianship. The Boom had ended, the dictators had come and gone, the magic realism had been replaced by bureaucratic realism. And yet, here was the past, literally at hand, demanding a form in triplicate.

He picked up his pen again. The story, it seemed, was not finished. It was just being filed.

Chapter 2

CHAPTER 2

By Thursday, Iñigo the Head had a routine. Mornings, he faced east, towards the mountains that had once hidden the General’s troops from the Royalist cavalry, now scarred with logging roads that led to abandoned mines. Don Eusebio believed in the symbolic importance of orientation. Afternoons, he was turned to face the plaza, so he could “participate in civic life,” which consisted of watching dogs sleep in the shade and the occasional municipal employee crossing from the bakery to the cantina at a pace that suggested profound regret. Evenings, he was covered with Remedios’s clean cloth, a white linen napkin embroidered with a faded crest that might have been a bunch of grapes or a cluster of grenades.

The ledger, however, remained stubbornly empty of meaningful data.

Wednesday. 9:08 a.m. No change in subject’s expression. Mustache still impeccable. Fly landed on left eyelid, remained for 4 minutes, departed without incident. Subject’s patience: commendable.

Wednesday. 2:30 p.m. Father Benítez visited. Read aloud from the Book of Ezekiel (“The valley of dry bones”). Subject unresponsive. Father expressed frustration, then guilt for frustration. Left muttering about metaphorical literalism.

Don Eusebio’s tremor worsened when he wrote about the head. It was as if the act of documenting the absurd made his body rebel against the premise. He was a man built for recording deeds and boundaries, for stamping and signing the tangible world. The head was a tangible object, yes, but it was an object that screamed of intangible things: fractured history, post-colonial fatigue, the ghost-limb ache of a missing body politic. It was too much metaphor for a notary’s hand.

The tension arrived, as it often did, on the wings of bureaucracy. A jeep the color of dried mud kicked up dust in the plaza, coming to a stop outside the church. A man in a khaki shirt with too many pockets emerged. He had the air of a man who carried official disappointment in a sealed envelope.

Capitán Rodrigo Páez of the Provincial Cultural Patrimony Office (a one-man office operating out of a converted petrol station two towns over) stood in the doorway of the Museum of National Memory, blocking the light. He held a clipboard.

“Don Eusebio. A report reached my desk. An irregular patrimonial acquisition.”

Don Eusebio’s heart performed a small, sinking maneuver. “Report? From whom?”

“The postal service logs all international parcels over five kilos. This one was five point two.” Capitán Páez spoke as if weight were a moral category. “Description was vague. ‘Historical artifact.’ You know the procedure. Form P-44 must be filed within 48 hours of receipt. I see no P-44.”

“It arrived unexpectedly,” Don Eusebio said, subtly sliding the ledger over the “Unexpected Patrimonial Acquisition” form he’d never finished. “I was in the process of authentication.”

“Authentication.” Páez savored the word, finding it wanting. He stepped in, his eyes performing an inventory of the room: the cracked glass cases holding rusted musket balls, the faded flag behind the desk that had been chewed by moths at the edges, the saint cards still scattered from the crate’s arrival. His gaze landed on the linen-draped shape on the desk. “And what is this?”

“A… conservation project.”

“Uncover it.”

There was no refusing. Don Eusebio lifted the napkin with the reverence of a priest unveiling a relic, which, he supposed, he was.

Capitán Páez stared. He blinked. He leaned closer. He did not gasp or exclaim. He was a bureaucrat; his surprises were internal, administrative. “That,” he said finally, “is a head.”

“It is.”

“It resembles General Íñigo de Alba y Merced.”

“The resemblance is uncanny.”

Páez straightened up, his mind already racing through procedural corridors. “This is a Category One Patrimonial Object. Arguably Category Zero. It cannot be here. This facility lacks climate control, security, the proper archival lighting. You have a gecko on the ceiling.”

“His name is Pepe,” Don Eusebio said, then instantly regretted it.

“This is not a joke, Notary. This head is the property of the nation. Its discovery—no, its delivery—must be handled with protocol. There will be papers. Committees. A chain of custody document stretching back to the moment of its… detachment.” He began writing furiously on his clipboard. “I am placing it under official seizure. It will be transported to the capital for proper analysis.”

A cold panic, sharper than any he’d felt, seized Don Eusebio. The head leaving was not just a loss of a curious problem; it felt like an amputation. Iñigo had been theirs for three days. They had named him. They had given him a view. They had begun the quiet, absurd work of caring for a piece of history everyone else had forgotten.

“The capital,” Don Eusebio whispered. “They’ll put him in a vault. Or a display case. They’ll charge admission.”

“As is proper for a national treasure,” Páez said, not looking up. “I will return with a proper container and a receipt at 1600 hours. Do not touch it. Do not… commune with it.” He gave Don Eusebio a look that suggested he knew, on some level, that communing had already occurred.

The jeep spat gravel as it left. Don Eusebio stood in the doorway, the weight of his helplessness as physical as the heat. He watched Remedios emerge from her cantina, wiping her hands on her apron. She had seen the jeep. She walked across the plaza, not hurrying, but covering the ground with the implacable momentum of a tide.

“Páez,” she stated, arriving.

“He’s taking him. To the capital.”

“Over my dead body,” she said, and it wasn’t a figure of speech. “Iñigo came to us. This is his post now. You don’t reroute the mail of the dead. It’s bad luck.”

“He has forms. He has a clipboard.”

“I have a soup ladle and a righteous fury.” But her eyes were calculating. “We need the priest. And we need a better argument than bad luck.”

Father Benítez was found in the confessional, though he was the one confessing, in a low murmur, to a statue of Saint Jude. “—and the doubt, it’s not a cloud, it’s the very air, you understand? To absolve a man who steals a chicken because his children are hungry is one thing. To absolve a society that keeps stealing his chicken, year after year, generation after generation, that is a theological knot I cannot—”

“Father,” Remedios interrupted, pulling back the curtain. “Crisis. The bureaucrat from Patrimony is stealing our head.”

Benítez emerged, looking relieved at the concrete heresy. “Stealing? It’s not ours to steal.”

“It was delivered into our care,” Don Eusebio insisted, hearing the weakness of the argument even as he made it. “We are the de facto committee.”

“Páez is de jure,” Benítez sighed. “The law is a ghost that haunts the living with the paperwork of the dead. The head belongs to the ghost.”

Remedios crossed her arms. “What if it doesn’t want to go?”

The two men looked at her.

“What if,” she continued, her voice dropping, “sending him away is against his… final wishes? He was a liberator. He fought central authority. He believed in local governance. The criollo spirit! Sending him to the capital, to some marble mausoleum, would be the ultimate betrayal. He’d be a trophy in the very palace he fought to dismantle.”

Don Eusebio felt a spark. It was historical nonsense, of course. The General had probably been a megalomaniac who wanted all the palaces for himself. But it was a narrative. A story with moral weight. “We could argue custodianship on historical-ethical grounds. That his legacy is best honored here, at the site of his first victory.”

“Where the statue is headless,” Benítez pointed out.

“A symbol of enduring struggle!” Don Eusebio’s tremor vanished in the heat of invention. “The body, decapitated but standing. The head, returning home. It’s… poetic. It’s Boom.”

“It’s desperate,” Benítez said, but a flicker of interest was in his eyes. He was a man who traded in symbols. “We would need evidence. A sign. Something to suggest the head’s… allegiance.”

They all looked at Iñigo. The head sat, impassive, a masterclass in waxen neutrality.

“We have until four o’clock,” Remedios said.

What followed was a montage of absurd research. Don Eusebio dug through the brittle archives, looking for the General’s will, his manifestos, any hint of a burial preference. He found only tax assessments and a furious letter complaining about the quality of imported saddle leather. Benítez scoured theological texts for precedents on the sanctity of final resting places, even for heretics and liberators. Remedios took a more direct approach. She brought Iñigo a small glass of aguardiente, the local firewater.

“If you’re one of us, you’ll appreciate this,” she said, holding it under his nose. “The good stuff. Not that capital swill.”

The head did not react. The aguardiente evaporated into the still air.

At 3:55, Don Eusebio stood before the head, ledger open. “This is your last chance,” he said, his voice tight. “A tear. A sigh. A twitch of the mustache. Give us something, Iñigo. We are your people. That man out there,” he gestured towards the window, where Páez’s jeep was pulling back into the plaza, “he sees a specimen. We see a… guest.”

The head offered only its serene, administrative disappointment.

Páez entered, carrying a plastic crate lined with foam. “Step aside, please.”

It was Benítez who moved, not aside, but forward. He placed himself between Páez and the desk. “Captain. A moment. Before you commit an act of historical violence.”

“Father, do not impede.”

“This head,” Benítez said, his voice taking on the sonorous tone of his Sunday sermons, “arrived not at a museum, or a ministry, but here. At the heart of the community he once freed. Is that not a message? In an age of disconnection, of centralized power sucking the marrow from the provinces, is this return not a symbol? A call to remember where we come from?”

Páez was unmoved. “My directive is to preserve the object. Symbolism has no climate control.” He reached for the head.

And then it happened.

A single, fat drop of clear liquid welled from the corner of the General’s closed right eye. It traced a slow, deliberate path down the waxy cheek, through the magnificent mustache, and hung, glistening, on the very tip of a silvered bristle.

Everyone froze.

Páez’s hand hovered in mid-air. “Is that… condensation?”

“It’s a tear,” Remedios whispered, triumph and awe in her voice.

“Impossible. It’s wax. It’s reacting to the humidity.” But Páez sounded less sure.

Don Eusebio, his hand steady for the first time in days, bent to the ledger. He wrote, his script bold and clear:

Thursday. 3:58 p.m. Subject exhibited lacrimation. Single tear, right eye. Context: imminent removal by patrimonial authority. Interpretation: protest.

He showed the entry to Páez.

The bureaucrat stared at the words, then at the tear, now falling onto the linen napkin, leaving a dark, star-shaped stain. He looked from the fervent face of the priest to the defiant posture of the widow to the notary’s meticulously recorded miracle. He was a man of forms, of categories. There was no form for this. Category: Anomalous Patriotic Lacrimation.

He lowered his hand. The plastic crate seemed suddenly cheap, insulting. “This… changes the procedural matrix,” he said stiffly. “I must… consult. This may require a sub-committee. A ruling from the central board on the treatment of… emotionally volatile patrimony.” He backed away, his clipboard held to his chest like a shield. “The head remains in situ. For now. But this incident will be reported. And monitored.”

He left, his retreat as orderly as a tactical withdrawal.

Silence flooded back into the room. The three of them stared at the head. The tear stain on the napkin was already drying.

“Did you…” Don Eusebio began, looking at Remedios.

“I didn’t touch him,” she said. Her eyes were wide. “I swear on my mother’s grave.”

Benítez made the sign of the cross, this time with genuine feeling. “Lacryma Christi,” he murmured. “Or very clever wax.”

Don Eusebio looked at Iñigo. The head’s expression was unchanged. Yet, something had shifted. The air in the room was charged, thick with the scent of cedar, aguardiente, and something else—the faint, electric smell of a story taking root. They had won a stay of execution with a tear. A miracle of bureaucracy.

He closed the ledger. The head was still theirs. But it was no longer just a head. It was a co-conspirator. And it had just spoken, in the only language their world understood: an ambiguous, perfectly-timed, administrative mystery.

Chapter 3

CHAPTER 3

The tear changed everything and nothing. It did not, as Father Benítez half-hoped, herald a new age of weeping statues and provincial revival. Nor did it, as Capitán Páez feared, indicate a systemic failure in national wax preservation standards. It simply was. A fact. An entry in the ledger.

But in a town where the most exciting event of the last decade had been the year the mangoes were unusually sweet, a single tear from a liberated head was a seismic event. It did not remain a secret. Remedios told the cook, who told the butcher, who told the woman who came to read the water meters, who had a cousin who worked the switchboard at the regional radio station. By Saturday, a faint crackle of static could be heard drifting from doorways, a radio announcer’s voice saying, “…unconfirmed reports of a mystical nature from the so-called Museum of National Memory in the town of…”

The first pilgrim arrived on Sunday. A bent old woman from the hills, leading a goat. She stood in the doorway of the annex, squinted at Iñigo on his desk, and asked, “Does he grant wishes?”

“He grants paperwork,” Don Eusebio replied, not looking up from the new form he was drafting: “P-44a: Supplemental Report on Spontaneous Lacrimation in Patrimonial Objects.”

“My son is in the city. He won’t write.” The woman’s voice was dry as husks. “Can the General make him write?”

Don Eusebio sighed. “Leave your name and a return address. We’ll add it to the petition file.”

He had created the “petition file” that morning—a folder where he placed the increasingly bizarre requests that were now whispered across his threshold. Cures for rheumatism. Intervention in land disputes. A blessing for a new truck. The head, a military man turned accidental saint, was being drafted into a new kind of service.

Remedios, seeing an opportunity, began selling little stamped medals from a basket by the cantina door. They were old St. Christopher medals she’d found in a trunk, the saint’s face rubbed smooth. “Touched to the General’s crate,” she’d say, winking. They sold for a few coins. It was commerce, but it was also a kind of protection, a way of weaving the head into the town’s daily economy, making it too valuable to lose.

Father Benítez watched this with deepening unease. His Sunday sermon was a tortured allegory about false idols and the dangers of sentimentalizing history. Half the congregation dozed; the other half glanced towards the annex, clearly thinking their pesos were better spent on Remedios’s medals than the collection plate.

The complication, when it came, did not arrive in a jeep. It arrived in a rust-savaged sedan that coughed to a stop in the plaza, releasing a man in a rumpled linen suit. He was not from the government. He was from the university. Professor Aníbal Sordo, Department of Post-Colonial Semiotics. He had a weary, handsome face and carried a leather satchel bulging with books and what smelled like a ripe mango.

He found Don Eusebio attempting to dust Iñigo’s ears with a soft brush.

“Ah,” Sordo said, his voice a pleasant baritone. “The objet petit mort.”

“I beg your pardon?”

“The little death object. The fetish that stands in for the unrepresentable trauma of the colonial aftermath.” Sordo smiled, a smile that had seen too many faculty meetings. “May I?” He didn’t wait for permission. He circled the head, peering at it not with Páez’s bureaucratic glare, but with a hungry, intellectual curiosity. “Fascinating. The waxwork is 19th century, but the preservation… that’s later. A 1920s technique, I’d wager. So he was preserved after his death, for display. A relic of the early Republic’s attempt to manufacture its own heroes. Then lost, or sold, or stolen. And now he returns. The repressed, literally, re-capitated.”

Don Eusebio felt a defensive pride rise in him. “He was sent to us.”

“Precisely! But why you? That is the question. Not to the national archive. Not to a museum in the capital. To a notary in a town whose only claim to fame is a headless statue.” Sordo’s eyes gleamed. “It’s a sublime joke. The center cannot hold, so the margins get the head. It’s a perfect text.”

“It’s not a text. It’s a… person.” The word slipped out before Don Eusebio could stop it.

Sordo’s smile widened. “Even better! You’ve anthropomorphized the national allegory. This is fieldwork gold.” He pulled a notebook from his satchel. “I’m writing a paper. ‘Cephalic Returns: The Head as Dialectical Image in the Late Neoliberal Landscape.’ This case is the centerpiece. I need to interview you. The committee. Document the rituals. The tear! The tear is a masterpiece. A collective psychic projection manifesting as saline condensation. Brilliant.”

Don Eusebio’s tremor returned. This man was more dangerous than Páez. Páez wanted to box the head. Sordo wanted to unpack it, to scatter its meaning across academic journals, to turn their quiet, absurd custody into a case study. He would use words like “hegemony” and “spectrality” and “the phantasmic body of the patria.” He would suck the life out of the mystery and leave the dry husk of a theory.

“We are not a case study,” Don Eusebio said, with as much dignity as he could muster.

“Everything is a case study, my friend. Especially the things that try not to be.” Sordo leaned in, confiding. “Look, I can help you. Páez is a clerk. I have influence. I can argue for the head to remain here as a ‘living historical site,’ a ‘site of ongoing popular hermeneutics.’ I can get you a grant. A small one. For a better shelf. A dehumidifier.”

The offer hung in the air. A dehumidifier. It was a powerful lure. Don Eusebio thought of the tear, of the waxy sheen in the afternoon heat. Proper conservation. It was their duty.

“What do you need?” he asked, his voice heavy.

“Access. To observe. To record. To understand the social dynamics the head generates. The petitions, the medals, the priest’s anxiety. It’s a perfect microcosm.” He looked at Iñigo. “He’s a catalyst. I want to study the reaction.”

That evening, the committee convened in the cantina’s back room, the air thick with the smell of fried plantains and stale beer. Iñigo was with them, placed respectfully on a barrel in the center, covered with his napkin. Professor Sordo was at the bar, ostensibly reading a book, his ears practically twitching.

“We can’t trust him,” Benítez hissed. “He’s a vampire. He’ll drain the meaning from everything and present it at a conference in Frankfurt.”

“He offers a dehumidifier,” Don Eusebio said. “And protection from Páez.”

“Protection?” Remedios snorted. “He’ll put us in a glass box next to the head. ‘Observe the native caretakers in their habitat.’” She took a long drink of beer. “But. The pilgrims will come. More of them. We need… systems. The medals are one thing. But what if someone wants to touch it? What if a real journalist comes? We need a story we can all tell.”

“We have a story,” Don Eusebio said. “The head came home.”

“It’s not enough. Why did it come home now?” Remedios’s eyes grew distant, strategic. “What if… it’s a sign? Not just a tear. A warning. Or a key.”

“A key to what?” Benítez asked, wary.

“To what the General left behind. He didn’t just fight. He governed. There were projects. Plans. Maybe… maybe he knew something. Hid something. And the head is the clue.”

Don Eusebio stared at her. She was constructing a narrative, a plot, right there in the cantina’s gloom. It was outrageous. It was perfect. It would give them purpose beyond custodianship. It would give the pilgrims a quest, not just a wish. It would give Sordo more than he bargained for.

“The Lost Projects of General Íñigo,” Don Eusebio murmured, the notary in him already imagining the file folders. “The Hydraulic Plan for the Valley. The Library of the Republic. The… the Cooperative of Indigenous Bee-keeping.”

“Bee-keeping?” Benítez looked pained.

“It’s plausible! He was an enlightened despot! It’s in the archives!” Don Eusebio felt a surge of creative energy. “We can research. We can ‘discover’ his unfinished visions. The head’s return signifies their time has come.”

“And the tear?” Sordo’s voice came from the bar. They all jumped. He closed his book, a faint smile on his lips. “Forgive me. The acoustics in here are remarkable. So. You’re moving from relic to roadmap. The head as a catalyst for utopian revival. I love it. It’s a beautiful, tragic, hopelessly romantic fiction.”

“It is not fiction,” Remedios said, her chin lifted. “It is a hypothesis.”

Sordo walked over, his gaze moving from their defiant faces to the napkin-covered shape on the barrel. “And what does the principal subject think?” With a dramatic flourish, he whisked off the napkin.

Iñigo sat, bathed in the cantina’s yellow light. The wax seemed softer here, warmer. In the dimness, the sewn-shut lips seemed less like preservation and more like enforced silence.

For a long moment, no one spoke. The weight of their invented conspiracy pressed down on the real, physical fact of the head.

Then, from outside, came the sound of the cathedral bell, tolling the hour. It was a broken sound, missing its deepest note since the earthquake. As it faded, a strange thing happened. A faint, almost imperceptible click echoed in the room. It seemed to come from the direction of the head.

They all froze.

Sordo leaned in, his academic detachment gone, replaced by raw curiosity. “Did it…?”

Don Eusebio looked at the head. Nothing had changed. And yet, everything had. The sound, real or imagined, had sealed their pact. The complication was no longer just an academic or a bureaucrat. The complication was the story itself, now growing, taking on flesh and bone and the faint scent of wax and hope. They had escalated the stakes from keeping a head to decoding a legacy, with a professor as their witness and a town waiting for a miracle.

The head sat, a silent partner in the conspiracy. Its return was no longer an end. It was a first sentence. And they had just written the second.

Don Eusebio reached for his notebook, not the official ledger, but a smaller, personal one. He wrote, his



---

Chapter 4

CHAPTER 4

The complication, as it turned out, had a name, a pension, and a flat above a haberdashery that smelled of mothballs and loneliness. Mr. Armitage did not look like a guardian of anything. He looked like a man who had been carefully folded and put away in a drawer for several decades, his edges softened by dust and disappointment. His suit was the color of weak tea, and his hands, when he opened the door to Alex, trembled with a fine, constant vibration, like a tuning fork struck long ago and never quite stilled.

“You’re late,” he said, his voice a dry rustle of paper. “By approximately seven hours and twenty-three minutes. The optimal confluence for a first consultation was at dawn. The veil is thinner then. It’s porous. Now we have only the residue.” He stepped back, allowing Alex to enter a sitting room so crowded with furniture and curios it felt less like a home and more like a storage facility for a museum that had lost its funding. Every surface held something under a bell jar or behind glass: a desiccated hummingbird, a clock with no hands, a single, yellowed glove.

Alex stood, still carrying the weight of the cantina, of the head’s impossible click, of the conspiratorial silence that had followed. They had come here because Don Eusebio, wiping his spectacles with a trembling handkerchief, had said it was the next logical step. “The head is a question,” he’d murmured. “This man… he deals in the grammar of questions.”

“Sit,” Armitage instructed, pointing to a wingback chair whose velvet was worn to a ghost of its original color. He did not sit himself, but paced a slow, precise circuit around the room, his fingers brushing certain objects as he passed—the edge of a fossil, the cool glass of a jar containing what looked like a cloudy, suspended eye. “You have been meddling with a focal object. A lodestone. You have attached a narrative to it, a rather sentimental and ambitious one. This has consequences. Narratives have mass. They bend things.”

“What things?” Alex asked. Their own voice sounded too loud, too young, in the muffled room.

“The weave,” Armitage said, stopping finally to look at them. His eyes were the pale grey of a winter sky, empty of warmth but full of a distant, assessing light. “You think the world is a solid, settled fact. A finished painting. It is not. It is a tapestry in a constant state of being mended, by indifferent hands, with thread that is often rotten. Your General’s head is not just a piece of political taxidermy. It is a knot. A snarl in the weave. And you, in your charming provincial fervor, have begun to pull at it.”

He moved to a large, dark globe that stood in the corner. It was not a map of the world, but of constellations Alex did not recognize, linked by silver lines that pulsed with a faint, internal light. “I am a Watcher. A somewhat grandiose title for a custodian of loose ends. My role is to observe the points where the weave frays, where the pattern threatens to unravel. To monitor the… colonial aftermath.” He said the last two words with a particular dryness, as if tasting a bitter pill. “Empires are not just political entities. They are violent assertions of one reality over another. When they fall, they leave scars. Not just on maps, but on the fabric of things. Rents. Tears. Your General was an agent of one such violent assertion. His preserved head is a scar that never healed. A tiny, persistent fault line.”

Alex felt the cantina’s bravado draining away, replaced by a cold, creeping clarity. “The sound it made. The click.”

“A tremor along the fault,” Armitage nodded. “You gave it a story of return, of legacy. You energized the scar. It is… responding.” He sighed, a sound like leaves skittering on stone. “Which means you are now involved. And I, due to a tedious and ancient compact, am now involved with you. So. We must see if you can be taught to see the tear, before you blunder into it and unravel your sleeve, or worse.”
  - The training was not what Alex had expected. There were no incantations, no glowing sigils drawn in the air. There was only the oppressive clutter of the flat and Armitage’s dry, relentless instruction.

“Perception is a habit,” he said, standing Alex before a blank stretch of wallpaper between a cabinet of butterflies and a stuffed badger. “You are in the habit of seeing what you expect to see. A wall. A badger. A dead insect. You must break the habit. Look sideways.”

For an hour, Alex stared at the wallpaper. They saw the faded pattern of lilies, a water stain shaped like a continent, a fly that had died and become part of the composition. Their eyes watered. Nothing happened.

“You’re trying too hard,” Armitage muttered, sipping from a cup of cold, unsweetened tea. “Stop looking. Receive.”

Frustration bubbled up in Alex’s throat. They thought of the head, of Remedios’s fierce hope, of Sordo’s amused detachment. They thought of the weight of the story they had all agreed to tell. They let their focus soften, their mind drift to the cantina’s yellow light, the smell of wax and spilled anise.

And then, the wallpaper shimmered.

It was not a visual effect so much as a feeling of wrongness, a suggestion of depth where there should be none. The lilies seemed to writhe for a second, their stems knotting into unfamiliar sigils. The water-stain continent bled at its edges, bleeding a faint, phosphorescent green. It lasted only a heartbeat, a trick of the light and tired eyes, but it left Alex breathless.

“Ah,” Armitage said, a note of grim satisfaction in his voice. “There. You saw the weave. Not the pattern printed on it, but the threads themselves. And you saw where the dye is unstable. That is a tear. A very small one. A memory of dampness, perhaps, or a forgotten argument that happened in this spot. Harmless. But proof of concept.”

He made Alex practice for two more hours. They learned to unfocus their eyes in a specific way, to let the solid world become slightly translucent, revealing the frantic, shimmering latticework beneath—the weave. It was beautiful and terrifying, a vast, living tapestry thrumming with energy. Most of it was strong, bright, and tight. But here and there were weak spots: dim patches, tangled knots, and occasionally, a jagged rip where the light of the underlying loom shone through, too bright and too cold.

“The tears are where other things bleed in,” Armitage explained as Alex traced the faint, shimmering outline of a rift near the ceiling, shaped like a screaming mouth. “Or bleed out. Memories, emotions, sometimes… entities. The aftermath. The colonial project was a sustained, concentrated effort to tear a new pattern into the existing weave. It did not take cleanly. The old pattern fights back. The scars weep.”
  - As the afternoon light faded into the thick, purple gloom of evening, Armitage ceased his pacing. He went to an ancient sea-chest, its wood scarred by salt and time, and unlocked it with a key that hung from a chain around his neck. From within, wrapped in faded indigo cloth, he drew out an object and placed it in Alex’s hands.

It was a compass. But its casing was tarnished silver, etched with the same unfamiliar constellations from his globe. The needle was not magnetic; it was a sliver of what looked like dark bone, suspended in a viscous, clear fluid that glowed with a soft, internal bioluminescence. It did not point north. As Alex held it, the bone needle quivered, spun lazily, and settled, pointing insistently toward the badger in the corner.

“A minor anomaly,” Armitage sniffed. “A residual melancholy. Very faint.” He took the compass back and gave it a sharp tap with his fingernail. The needle spun again, then drifted and steadied, pointing now toward the window, in the general direction of the city park. “This is more like it. An ancestral compass. It doesn’t care about poles. It points toward dissonance. Toward instability in the weave. The stronger the pull, the larger or more active the rift.”

Alex turned the cool metal over in their hands. “Ancestral?”

“It was your great-grandmother’s,” Armitage said, turning to adjust a dial on his strange globe. “She was a Watcher, too. A better one than I. The bone in the needle is hers. A familial link often enhances sensitivity. She would be appalled at the current state of the thresholds. As am I.”

The weight of the object changed instantly in Alex’s grasp. It was no longer just a tool. It was an inheritance. A verdict. They thought of their own family, a vague, modern constellation of quiet people who worked in offices and worried about real estate. There had been no stories of great-grandmothers who watched the weave of reality.

“Why me?” Alex asked, the question finally surfacing. “Why now?”

Armitage did not look at them. He watched the needle, which was now vibrating slightly, emitting a faint, almost musical hum. “Because the head is here. And you are here. And the narrative you’ve spun around it is a thread that has, quite by accident, looped around a loose end that needs tying off. Causality is not a line. It is a net. You are caught in it. The compass may help you navigate. Or it may simply confirm your entrapment.”
  - He sent them out at a quarter to midnight. The instructions were precise, delivered in that rustling, paper-dry voice: “Go to the park. The compass will guide you to the epicenter of the disturbance. It is a minor rift, a seepage. Likely a concentration of forgotten pains—a dueling ground, perhaps, or a place where they buried paupers before the city expanded. Your task is not to close it. That is beyond you. Your task is to witness it. To feel its texture. To place a temporary patch upon it.”

From his chest, he produced a small pouch of what he called “quiet dust”—a mixture of powdered moonstone, salt from a dried-up ancient sea, and the ashes of certain silence-loving moths. “Sprinkle it across the tear’s aperture. It will mute the seepage for a time. Calm the local… atmosphere. Report back to me what you see, what you feel. Do not touch the rift itself. Do not speak to anything that may emerge. You are an observer. A temporary custodian. Nothing more.”

The streets were quiet, the town sunk in its familiar, broken sleep. The cathedral bell did not toll; the hour passed in silence. Alex walked, the compass a cold, living weight in their pocket. Its pull was a tangible thing now, a gentle, insistent tugging toward the iron gates of the municipal park, which were always left unlocked, a testament to a town with nothing left to steal.

The park at night was a different organism. The daytime smells of cut grass and children’s laughter were gone, replaced by the damp scent of earth and rotting leaves. The statues of forgotten mayors and poets loomed like pale ghosts in the gloom. Alex took out the compass. The bone needle glowed brighter, the fluid pulsing with a soft, blue light. It pointed unerringly down a path of cracked asphalt, toward the old bandstand, a wooden structure that had long since lost its music.

Following the pull, Alex practiced the sideways sight. The world softened, the solid trees becoming gauzy silhouettes against the vibrant, shimmering weave of the park’s underlying reality. It was mostly a peaceful pattern, a slow, green-gold hum of growth and decay. But ahead, near the bandstand, the pattern snarled.

A rip hung in the air, about the size of a door. It was not a hole into nothingness, but a jagged, shimmering wound in the tapestry. Through it, Alex could see not another place, but a distorted version of this one. The bandstand in the rift was new, painted white, and bathed in the harsh, yellow light of gas lamps. Figures in old-fashioned military uniforms, their faces blurred smudges of pride and fear, stood rigidly. And lying on the bandstand’s steps was a shape, dark and liquid. It was a memory, bleeding through. A forgotten execution. A colonial officer shot for disobedience, or for mercy, the record lost. The emotional residue—the shock, the injustice, the final, cold release—seeped out like a miasma, making the air around the real bandstand feel thick and charged with a sorrow so old it had turned to stone.

This was the colonial aftermath. Not in history books, but here, vibrating in the air, a silent, screaming scar on the world’s skin.

Hands trembling, Alex approached. The pull from the compass was strong now, a magnetic yearning toward the pain. They could hear things, not with their ears, but in the bones of their skull: a muffled order, a single, sharp report, the sound of a body falling. They fumbled for the pouch of quiet dust.

As they did, a figure detached itself from the deeper shadows of the bandstand. Not a memory from the rift, but solid, present. It was Sordo. He leaned against a post, watching Alex, his face unreadable in the dark. In his hand, he held not a book, but a small, dark notebook.

“The acoustics in here are remarkable, aren’t they?” he said, his voice quiet, carrying no trace of its usual academic amusement. “You can still hear the echoes. Fainter than the bell, but much, much older.”

Alex froze, the pouch half-open. “What are you doing here?”

“Following a hypothesis,” Sordo said, pushing off the post and walking closer. He glanced at the compass in Alex’s hand, at the shimmering tear they were both now able to see. He showed no surprise. Only a deep, weary recognition. “Don Eusebio is a notary. He believes in documents, in ledgers. Remedios is a revolutionary. She believes in futures. You, it seems, are inheriting a different kind of ledger. And I,” he sighed, “I am a historian of silence. I study the things that are not said, the events that leave only a stain. Like this one.” He gestured toward the weeping rift.

He stepped closer, peering at the tear with the keen interest of a scholar examining a rare manuscript. “So. The General’s head is a knot. And you are learning to untangle knots. How… extraordinarily inconvenient for everyone.” He looked at Alex, and his smile returned, but it was thin, sharp, and devoid of warmth. “My hypothesis was that the head was merely a symbol. A catalyst for human folly or ambition. It seems my hypothesis was incomplete. The symbol itself is… active.”

Before Alex could respond, Sordo reached out, not toward the rift, but toward the compass. His fingers hovered over the bone needle, which began to spin wildly. “An interesting device. It doesn’t just find tears, does it? It finds potential. Energy. A story like the one we’re telling… that’s a tremendous amount of potential energy.” He looked from the compass to the tear, then back to Alex. “Tell me, what did your Watcher say would happen if you pulled too hard on a loose thread?”

The quiet dust was forgotten in Alex’s fist. The rift pulsed, the memory of the gunshot echoing again, louder this time. Sordo’s presence was a new weight, a new variable in the equation. He was no longer just a witness. He was here, in the dark, at the site of a wound, asking the precise, dangerous questions of a man who has stopped being amused and has started to calculate.

The bone needle, agitated by his proximity and the rising energy of the scene, gave a sudden, violent jerk. It spun away from the bandstand rift and pointed, with unwavering certainty, back toward the town. Back toward the three-room annex behind the only functioning church. Back toward the General’s head.

The tear in the park was a whisper. The pull from the Museum was now a scream. Something had changed. The story had not just grown. It had begun to pull back.

Chapter 5

CHAPTER 5

The weeping rift in the bandstand was a small, ugly thing. It was the kind of wound a town learns to ignore, like a persistent ache in a bad tooth or the memory of a debt that can never be repaid. Alex’s hands, which had known only the dry textures of paper and the cool smoothness of the compass, felt absurd before it. The quiet dust was a pathetic counterweight to a tear in the world.

But the Watcher’s instructions were a series of precise, cold motions, a suturing technique for reality. Alex did not pull the thread. Instead, with a focus that burned away Sordo’s unsettling presence, they began to trace it. The compass needle, still shivering with the pull from the Museum, was forced to obey. Its bone tip became a guide, following the frayed edge of the memory—the gunshot, the panic, the singular, stupid moment of violence that had, for a century, refused to be digested. The act was not one of force, but of acknowledgment. I see you, the tracing said. I name you. You are here, and then you are done.

The rift did not heal with a sigh. It unspooled. The sound of the gunshot played once more, crisp and final, and then it was simply a sound, divorced from its consequence. The shimmering air tightened, puckered, and sealed over with a faint snick, like the closing of a locket containing nothing of value. The damp chill lifted. The bandstand was just a bandstand again, wood rotting under a neutral moon.

A thin, clear resin coated Alex’s fingertips where they had guided the energy. It smelled of ozone and, faintly, of cordite. Their first seal. It felt less like a victory and more like the correction of a typo in a vast, grim text. But in the marrow of their bones, a new understanding clicked into place: the world was not solid. It was a consensus, fragile and perpetually under negotiation.

Sordo, who had observed the entire process with the detached curiosity of a man watching a spider repair its web, let out a soft, appreciative breath. “Elegant,” he murmured. “You don’t erase the memory. You simply… give it a period where there was once a scream. A historian’s solution, after all.” He patted his coat pockets, producing a silver case from which he extracted a thin cigarette. He did not offer one. “But your punctuation mark has only agitated the rest of the sentence.”

He was right. The compass, now freed from the local disturbance, became a living thing in Alex’s palm. It did not spin. It wrenched. The bone needle tore itself toward the northern edge of town, toward the dark mass of the hills, with such ferocity that the brass rim grew warm. The pull was no longer a scream from the Museum. It was a seismic shift, a gravitational lure. The General’s head was a silent, rotting sun, and this was a planet caught in its sudden, violent orbit.

“The observatory,” Alex said, the words tasting of the Watcher’s dry lessons. “It’s coming from the old observatory.”

Sordo’s match flared, carving hollows into his face. “Of course it is,” he said, the smoke curling around the words like ivy around a tombstone. “A place built to watch silent, distant lights. How perfect for a silence that wishes to be seen.” He fell into step beside Alex as they moved, not as a companion, but as a parallel phenomenon, a shadow with its own distinct agenda. “They built it with a grant from the federal capital, you know. A gesture of enlightenment for the backward provinces. The telescope arrived rusted in its crate. They never saw a thing.”

The walk out of the town’s grid and into the clutching darkness of the hills was a journey out of one century and into the carcass of another. The observatory was a pale, domed blister on the hill’s brow, a folly of optimism. Its copper roof had long since verdigrised into a sickly green; its columns were strangled by creepers that seemed to pulse in the low light. The great door, a proud arch of oak, was splintered and ajar, a mouth frozen in a surprised gasp.

Inside, the silence was not an absence, but a substance. It was the silence of a vacuum bell jar, thick and resistant. Dust motes hung in the stale air, not drifting, but suspended, as if time here had been poured into jelly. The grand telescope, a colossus of brass and ambition, lay on its side like a felled giant, its lens a cataract eye staring at nothing. The domed ceiling, once meant to rotate toward the heavens, was cracked open in a jagged star-shape, letting in a smear of night sky.

And in the center of the main floor, where a meridian circle should have been etched, the world was wrong.

This was not a weeping rift. This was a maw.

It hovered a foot above the cracked marble, a vertical pool of inverted light. It did not shimmer. It gnawed. The edges were not frayed threads, but jagged, crystalline teeth of solidified paradox, chewing silently at the fabric of the room. Through it, Alex could see not a memory, but a place. A twisted, charcoal sketch of the observatory, where the telescope was a spiny, petrified creature and the dome was a cage of ribs. The air that breathed from it was hot and carried a scent of hot stone and spoiled honey.

“Ah,” Sordo breathed, his scholarly detachment finally cracking into something akin to greed. “A stable aperture. Or semi-stable. This isn’t a memory leak. This is a… a trade route.”

The compass was a live coal, the needle fused in a straight line toward the portal. The pull was physical now, a hook in Alex’s sternum. This was the source. This was the knot the General’s head had tied in the world’s lining.

“It needs to be closed,” Alex said, their voice swallowed by the thick air.

“Closed?” Sordo chuckled, a dry, papery sound. “My dear child. You don’t ‘close’ a door like this. You study it. You catalogue what comes through.” He took a step closer, peering into the inverted light. “The colonial project was not just about taking gold and land. It was an attempt to impose one reality upon another. A brutal, clumsy stitching. When the needle pulls out, the tear doesn’t always heal. Sometimes it becomes a… a membrane. A place where the story that was meant to be, and the story that was, rub raw against each other.”

As he spoke, the substance of the portal rippled. From the nightmarish sketch of the other side, something detached. It was not a shape, but a negation of shape, a lump of distorted matter that moved with a terrible, viscous purpose. It was the color of a bruise in twilight, and it seemed to absorb the faint light from the broken dome, growing denser and more defined as it passed through the crystalline teeth of the rift.

It had too many legs, or perhaps they were arms, articulated in all the wrong places. Its surface shifted like clay under a sculptor’s frantic hand, never settling on a form. It was a creature of unresolved history, of consequence without cause. A thing born from the wound.

It landed on the marble with a soft, wet sound. The suspended dust around it curdled and fell. It turned, not with a head, but with a focal point of profound cold, toward Alex. Toward the compass. Toward the active, stitching energy it represented.

Sordo took a sharp step backward, his calculation instantly overriding his curiosity. “Fascinating,” he whispered, no longer to Alex, but to himself. “The pressure differential… it creates manifest corrections.”

The thing scuttled forward. Its movement was a series of jarring teleports, an inch here, a foot there, the space between its positions glitching with afterimages. It wasn’t walking. It was editing itself closer.

Panic, cold and pure, flushed through Alex. The quiet dust was useless. The suturing technique was for tears, for memories. This was a thing. A thing that was here, now, and its intention was a hollow, hungry void.

Alex stumbled back, fumbling with the compass. The Watcher had spoken of guardians, of echoes that took on protective form, but this was no guardian. This was a tumor.

A tendril, sharp as a shard of obsidian, lashed out from the shifting mass, whistling through the air where Alex’s throat had been a moment before. It struck the base of the fallen telescope, and the brass screamed, shearing away in a ribbon of metal that dissolved into black smoke before it hit the floor.

The pull from the portal was a siren song, a violent tug. The creature advanced, its form now suggesting a multitude of grasping hands, now a single, gaping mouth. Sordo had vanished into the deeper shadows by the wall, a spectator to a duel he had no stake in.

Another lash. This one caught Alex’s coat sleeve, and the wool didn’t tear—it unraveled, chronologically, the threads reverting to raw sheep’s wool before dissipating into nothing. A wave of nullifying cold shot up Alex’s arm.

There was no time. No plan. The Watcher’s lessons were a distant lecture. The creature was between Alex and the only door. The only other exit was the wound itself.

The compass, burning with sympathetic agony, seemed to pulse with the portal’s rhythm. Pull. Pull. Pull.

The thing gathered itself, a mound of blasphemous physics ready to spring.

Alex made the only choice the story left them. They stopped fighting the pull.

They ran toward the maw.

They leaped, not away from the creature, but into the inverted light, into the jagged, crystalline teeth of the rift. It was not an escape. It was a surrender to the narrative gravity.

The world did not go dark. It unfolded.

There was a sound like a million pages being torn from a million books at once. The observatory—the real, solid, broken observatory—stretched, smeared, and snapped away like a rubber band. The sensation was of being plunged into a vat of lukewarm oil and shattered glass. Pressure, immense and directionless, flattened Alex’s lungs. Light did not illuminate; it existed in solid, contradictory planes. They saw the silhouette of the creature, frozen mid-lunge at the rim of the portal, which now looked like a tiny, shivering coin of normalcy receding at the end of a long, warped tunnel.

Then they were through.

They landed on a surface that had the texture of rough paper and the give of moss. The air was breathable, but it carried a metallic taste, like the memory of blood. The silence was absolute, a vacuum that pressed against the eardrums.

Alex pushed themselves up, retching dryly. They were in the observatory. And they were not.

It was a charcoal sketch given dimension. Everything was rendered in shades of gray, silver, and drained umber. The dome overhead was indeed a cage of petrified ribs, curving up into a sky that was not a sky, but a ceiling of roiling, silent storm clouds the color of lead and old ash. The fallen telescope was not brass, but a spine of black, porous stone, grown over with lichen that glowed a faint, sickly yellow. The walls were not stone, but something like compressed, stratified dust, etched with faint, ghostly patterns that might have been maps of constellations that never existed.

This was the Echo Realm. Not a memory, but the negative space around the story. The world’s discarded draft.

And it was not empty.

Figures moved in the middle distance, near the twisted telescope. They were human-shaped, but rendered in smudges of charcoal, their features blurred as if by a careless thumb. They went through motions—carrying phantom instruments, pointing at the ribbed dome, consulting ethereal charts—in a silent, endless loop. The astronomers. The echo of their labor, their futile watch for lights that would never come, trapped in the place where lost purposes went to repeat themselves.

Alex’s compass lay beside them. The bone needle was spinning slowly, lazily, as if disoriented. It no longer pointed. It searched.

A new sound began, cutting through the profound silence. A slow, wet, tearing noise.

Alex turned.

The portal, their way back, hung in the air behind them. But its crystalline teeth were… healing. Knitting together with a substance like black coral. And on the other side, pressed against the closing aperture like a face against glass, was the shifting, bruise-colored form of the creature. It had not followed. It was guarding. It would not let the thread be pulled back through.

They were trapped in the Echo.

And then, a different figure resolved itself from the gray strata of the wall. It stepped away from the stone as if parting a curtain, its form sharper, more defined than the smudged astronomers. It wore the remnants of a military uniform from a forgotten regiment, bleached of all color. Its face was a smooth, blank oval, devoid of features. But in its hands, it carried something familiar, something that sent a new, deeper chill into Alex’s soul.

It carried a small, ornate crate, exuding a faint, familiar scent of cedar and formalin.

The blank face seemed to regard Alex. It did not speak. It had no mouth with which to speak. It simply held the crate, a silent custodian of the very thing that had started it all, here in the realm of consequences. The General’s head was not just a knot in the real world. Here, it was an artifact. A relic. A focal point.

The compass needle gave a final, decisive shudder. It stopped spinning. It pointed, with absolute clarity, at the crate in the featureless sentinel’s hands.

The hook was not in Alex’s sternum anymore. It was in the very ground beneath them, in the air they breathed. They had not escaped the story by crossing over. They had jumped directly into its source code. The pulling had stopped. Now, they were simply here, a live variable in the silent, grinding equation of colonial aftermath, and the solution was waiting for them to pick it up.



---

Chapter 6

CHAPTER 6

Alex’s consciousness returned not with a jolt, but with the slow, viscous insistence of oil separating from water. They were lying on a surface that was neither stone nor soil, but something that gave like packed ash and hummed with a residual, sub-audible frequency—the memory of a vibration. The gray sky, if it was a sky, hung low and featureless, a dome of unpolished pewter. The air carried no scent, only a pressure, a weight against the eardrums like the moments before a thunderclap that never arrives.

The compass was a cold, dead weight against their chest. The needle was motionless, a sliver of obsidian aimed at a fixed point in the middle distance. The crate, and its blank-faced custodian, were gone. The portal was a memory of a scar, now healed over with the strange, black-coral substance. They were alone in a silence so complete it had texture, a woolen density.

They pushed themselves up. Their limbs moved with a strange resistance, as if the atmosphere itself were thicker here, more reluctant. Looking down, they saw their own hands were unchanged, solid, real. A paradox. They were the most definite thing in this indefinite place, and the wrongness of that was a cold stone in their gut.

A voice, dry and cracked as old leather, came from behind them.

“You’re leaking.”

Alex turned, too slowly. The figure leaning against a formation that resembled a frozen waterfall of soot was tall, gaunt, dressed in patched layers of cloth the color of dust and dried clay. His skin was pale, etched with fine lines that spoke of squinting into barren distances. He held a long, notched staff of some dark wood. His eyes were the most startling thing: a sharp, lucid gray, the color of the realm itself, but alive with a weary, calculating intelligence. He was looking not at Alex’s face, but at their feet.

Alex looked down. Where they stood, the ashen ground was darkening, beading with tiny droplets of something that was not water. It was a deeper gray, almost black, and it fell with a slow, gravitational pull that seemed personal. Each drop hit the ground and did not soak in. It rested on the surface, a tiny stain of elsewhere.

“You’re from the other side,” the man said. It wasn’t a question. He pushed off from the rock with a sigh that seemed to carry the weight of a thousand minor disappointments. “Kael. And you’re a problem. Problems that leak through the rifts usually get mopped up by the Gloom before I have to look at them. You’re late.”

Alex found their voice. It sounded too loud, too coarse in the muffled world. “Where is this?”

Kael’s bark of laughter was short, humorless. “The Echo. The after-party. The place where everything your world pushed out finally lands.” He gestured vaguely with his staff at the desolate, stratified landscape. “All the forgotten treaties, the un-mourned dead, the ghost-ships of bad conscience. The metaphysical runoff. Used to be more organized. Had a kind of… geometry. A logic. Now it’s just this.” He nudged a loose piece of scree with his foot. It crumbled into a fine powder that drifted sideways, defying the absent wind. “Decaying.”

“Decaying into what?”

“Into nothing. Into the Gloom. And into your world, through the cracks.” Kael’s sharp eyes finally met Alex’s. “Your ‘sudden’ storms. Your buildings that develop cancers in the plaster. Your clocks that run backward on certain Tuesdays. That’s us. Our rot, bleeding through. You’re welcome.”

He began to walk, not waiting, his staff making soft thumps in the ash. Alex, with no other option, followed. The leaking from their soles had ceased, leaving a trail of dark, perfect beads behind them like a morbid breadcrumb trail.

“You said a heartstone,” Alex ventured, struggling to match his loping, economical stride.

Kael didn’t break pace. “The Heartstone. Capital. The… anchor. The thing that gave this place its shape and kept the tides of consequence in check. A focusing lens. Stolen.”

“By who?”

“By a man with a damp mustache and no courage. By a retired notary with a tremor. By a system that files atrocities under ‘Miscellaneous.’” Kael’s voice was flat. “It crossed over, packaged in cedar and chemical, and it took our center with it. Now the Echo unravels. And the Gloom feasts on the loose threads.”

As if summoned by the name, the quality of the light changed. It didn’t dim so much as it curdled. The uniform gray of the sky bruised in a specific quadrant, a bloom of ochre and deep violet. The air grew colder, and the pressure against Alex’s ears intensified. A sound began, not a growl or a shriek, but a low, resonant vibrato, like a cello string bowed by a numb hand.

Kael stopped. His knuckles were white on his staff. “Run. Now.”

“What is—”

“The Gloom doesn’t is. It consumes. Run!”

He took off, not in a panic, but with a desperate, ground-eating efficiency. Alex ran after him. The curdled light pursued, flowing over the terrain like a stain spreading through cloth. From within it, shapes began to coalesce. They were not bodies, but suggestions of motion—a limb that was all joint and no muscle, a maw that was a vortex of shadow, a swarm of grasping, filament-thin tendrils that snagged on the rocks. They moved with a sickening, stop-motion jerkiness, here then there, eating the distance between.

Kael led them into a canyon of the layered, sedimentary rock. The walls were close, etched with faint, swirling patterns that might have been fossilized currents. The Gloom poured into the mouth of the canyon behind them, the vibrato rising to a teeth-aching whine.

“They’re herding us!” Kael shouted over the noise.

The canyon dead-ended in a sheer wall of the black-coral substance, smooth and impassable. They were trapped. Kael turned, planting his staff, his face a mask of grim resignation. The Gloom filled the canyon mouth, a churning wall of hungry negation. The lead entities detached, skittering forward on too many limbs.

Alex’s mind, cold with fear, did not go blank. It clicked. This place had rules, just different ones. The resistance in the air, the way the powder had drifted sideways, the humming ground. It was all malleable. It was consequence without cause, effect seeking form. They remembered the compass needle, spinning, searching. They remembered the feeling of the hook, not in their flesh, but in the fabric of things.

They didn’t think. They reached.

Not with their hands, but with the part of them that had felt the pull of the General’s head, that had crossed the threshold. They reached for the substance of the Echo itself—for the pressure, for the resistance, for the hum in the ground.

And it answered.

The air in front of the skittering entities thickened. It wasn’t a wall, but a sudden zone of profound density. The creatures hit it and slowed, their jerky motions becoming a agonizing crawl, as if moving through hardening cement. The vibrato sound distorted, deepening to a frustrated groan.

Kael’s head snapped toward Alex, his eyes wide. “What are you—?”

Alex didn’t hear him. Their focus was on the canyon wall beside them. The layered rock. They pushed their will against it, not to break it, but to persuade it. The memory of a current, fossilized in the stone. The suggestion of a different arrangement.

With a sound like a vast book being riffled, the strata of the wall shifted. A section of it slid sideways, rearranging itself, opening a narrow, crooked fissure that hadn’t been there a second before. It led into darkness.

“Go!” Alex gasped, the effort a cold fire in their veins.

Kael didn’t hesitate. He dove into the fissure. Alex stumbled after, and as they crossed the threshold, they released their hold on the thickened air. A wave of exhaustion hit them, followed by a surge of nauseating disorientation. Behind them, they heard the Gloom crash into the space they’d vacated, the vibrato spiking into a shriek of thwarted hunger.

They collapsed in the dark, breathing hard. After a moment, a soft, phosphorescent fungus on the wall of the new passage glowed to life, revealing Kael staring at them. His earlier cynicism was gone, replaced by a wary, calculating awe. He looked at Alex as if seeing them for the first time.

“You didn’t just leak through,” he said, his voice hushed. “You brought a piece of the hook with you. Didn’t you?”

Alex, still trembling from the exertion, could only nod.

Kael leaned back against the wall, a slow, grudging smile touching his lips. It didn’t reach his tired eyes. “Well. That changes the arithmetic.” He nodded down the phosphorescent tunnel. “The Gloom can’t follow here. The stone is still… obedient. For now.” He looked back at Alex. “You can bend the Echo. You made a door.”

“I made a crack,” Alex corrected, their voice raw.

“Same thing, here.” Kael fell silent for a long moment, studying the patterns in the glowing fungus. “The Heartstone. Your people called it something else. They packed it in sawdust and formalin and called it evidence. They filed it away and called it history.” He looked at Alex, his gray eyes piercing in the faint light. “It’s not a knot in your world, leak. It’s the keystone in ours. And you, with that hook in your soul… you might be the only one who can find where they buried it. Not in your world. But where the weight of it sits here.”

He pushed himself up, extending a hand to pull Alex to their feet. His grip was strong, his skin cool.

“The blank sentinels guard the relics,” Kael said. “The Gloom consumes the decay. And I… I navigate the ruins. So, leak. You need a guide. And I,” he sighed, the weight of a thousand minor disappointments returning to his voice, “apparently need a lever. A live variable.”

He turned and started down the tunnel. “The sentinel you saw, with the crate. It was a keeper. There are others. They stand at the loci, the points of greatest historical… density. Where the theft left the deepest wound.” He glanced back, his profile sharp in the gloom. “Your compass. It points to the wound, doesn’t it?”

Alex touched the cold metal through their shirt. It pointed unerringly ahead, down the tunnel Kael was leading them into.

“It does now,” Alex said.

“Good,” Kael said, and there was no comfort in the word. “Then it’s not just pointing at the past. It’s pointing at the only future this place has left. We follow the pain. And we hope what’s at the end of it can still be lifted.”

The phosphorescent light stretched their shadows ahead of them, long and thin, like needles themselves seeking a vein. The silence of the Echo closed in again, but it was a different silence now. It was the silence of a held breath, of a equation waiting for its final term to be entered. Alex walked, the dead compass a pendulum against their heart, following a guide who smelled of dust and resignation, into the mapped, interior wound of a world they had been pulled into. The pulling had stopped. Now, the walking began. And somewhere ahead, in the silent, stratified dark, the General’s head waited in its crate, not as a relic of what was done, but as the still-beating heart of what was left undone.



---

Chapter 7

CHAPTER 7

The resistance camp was not a camp, but a slow collapse held at bay by stubbornness and string. It occupied the fossilized ribcage of something immense, a curvature of bone-white stone that rose from the floor of a cavern like the fingers of a buried hand refusing to be interred. The phosphorescence here was not a fungus, but a seepage, a viscous, amber light that wept from the ceiling and pooled in the hollows of the ancient vertebrae, giving everything the glazed look of an insect trapped in resin.

Kael led Alex into the bowl of this skeletal palm with the air of a man returning a lost, slightly problematic item. The air smelled of damp wool, of mushroom broth, and underneath, the sharp, mineral scent of fear sweat that had long since dried into the cloth and the skin.

“Don’t expect speeches,” Kael muttered, his voice barely disturbing the thick air. “Or uniforms. We’re less a resistance and more of a persistent stain. An inconvenient smudge on the ledger.”

The inhabitants were fewer than Alex had imagined, and quieter. A dozen figures moved in the sepia gloom with the economical gestures of people conserving momentum. Some were like Kael, their features sharp and their coloration muted, as if they’d been drawn with charcoal on old paper. Others were unmistakably human, but a specific kind of human: their clothes were wrong—a doublet here, a nylon tracksuit top there—and their eyes held the flat, polished sheen of river stones, worn smooth by the constant flow of unreality.

One of them, a woman with hair the color of tarnished silver and hands stained with something that looked like ink or old blood, looked up from mending a boot with catgut. Her gaze moved from Kael to Alex, and settled on the slight bulge beneath Alex’s shirt where the compass lay.

“Another leak,” she stated. Her voice was a dry rustle, like pages turning in a forgotten book.

“This one’s compass-locked,” Kael said, as if diagnosing a condition. “Points true. To the loci.”

The woman’s needle paused. “Does it now.” It wasn’t a question. She returned to her stitching. “Vance will have felt the pull. A new needle, drawn to his magnet. He’ll be pleased. He likes it when the tools deliver themselves.”

Alex felt the words like a cold draft. “Who is Vance?”

The woman bit the catgut thread. “The curator,” she said. Then she smiled, a thin, humorless stretching of lips. “The archaeologist. The colonial administrator. Pick the metaphor that fits the hole in your history. He’s the man who picked the lock on your world and decided our relics looked better on his shelf.”

A man emerged from a lean-to propped against a curved rib. He was human, or had been, but one side of his face was blurred, as if a thumb had smudged wet charcoal across his cheek and jaw. He moved with a limp, but his eyes were clear and terribly tired.

“Kael. You’re back.” His voice was a soft baritone, worn at the edges. “And you’ve brought the variable.”

“Elian,” Kael said, with a nod that was the camp’s version of a salute. “This is Alex. The compass points. They saw the sentinel-keeper at the Municipal Palace locus.”

Elian’s smudged face did something complicated. “The General’s crate.”

“Yes.”

Elian looked at Alex for a long moment. “You saw it? Not a memory. The actual crate, in the Echo?”

“It was… leaking,” Alex said, fumbling for the vocabulary of this place. “Sand. And the sentinel was guarding it. Adding more seals.”

“Preserving the scene of the crime,” Elian said softly. “The crime itself is the relic now.” He gestured for them to sit on a nearby outcropping. “Vance,” he began, and then stopped, assembling the story like a man picking shards of glass out of a wound. “Vance was a historian. Or an antiquarian. Or a thief. The categories bled together for him. He found a crack, a weak suture between our world and yours. Not a leak, like you. A surgeon. He made an incision.”

Elian explained with the calm cadence of a notary reading a damning clause. The Heartstone was not a gem, or a weapon. It was a lodestone, but for coherence. It was the quiet hum at the center of the Echo that kept the memories from dissolving into screaming chaos, that allowed the past to sediment in layers rather than swirl in a mad soup. It was the keystone, as Kael had said, that kept the archive from becoming a furnace.

“Vance didn’t understand its function,” Elian said. “He understood its value. Its singularity. He saw it as the ultimate prize, the one artifact that had no duplicate in any world. A decade ago, he cut it from its chamber in the Citadel of Echoes—the place where the realm’s… metabolism is most concentrated. He took it back through his incision to your side.”

“And the Gloom?” Alex asked, thinking of the devouring blackness in the square.

“Is the infection,” Kael said from the shadows. “The rot that sets in when the center cannot hold. The Heartstone maintained the balance. It let things rest. Without it, the past doesn’t rest. It putrefies. The Gloom consumes that putrefaction, but it’s a hungry fire. It doesn’t stop at the rot. It eats the walls, the floor, the very idea of the place.”

“Vance uses it,” the silver-haired woman, who was named Lyra, said without looking up from her mending. “He’s made a battery of our decay. The Gloom is his weapon. He directs it. Clears out areas that resist him. He’s not just a thief. He’s a plantation owner. This is his factory farm, and the crop is entropy.”

Alex’s hand went to the compass. It was cold and still. “He’s here now?”

“He comes and goes,” Elian said. “But his seat of power is the Citadel. He sits in the plundered heart, using a fraction of the Stone’s power to stabilize his little fiefdom, and vents the rest of the imbalance out as the Gloom to scour us away. He’s building something there. A permanent bridge. A way to move more than just ideas and memories. To move material. Armies, perhaps. Or simply inventory.”

The image formed in Alex’s mind, cold and precise: a man in a well-appointed study, a stolen world-heart pulsing on his desk, using its light to read maps of conquest, while outside his window, the world he was stealing from dissolved into screaming shadows.

“We have to take it back,” Alex said. The words felt absurd, childishly direct in the face of such systemic, elegant theft.

Lyra barked a laugh. “Oh, good. The leak has a plan.”

“We do have to take it back,” Elian said, quieting her with a look. “But the Citadel is… defended. Not just by sentinels. By the landscape itself. The canyon that leads to it is not a canyon of rock.
